Either way, it's suspenseful enough that I can easily look past the flaws and highly recommend it.

1451627289|5.0|When I first read the basic premise for this book, ie, some guy goes back in time to try to kill Lee Harvey Oswald before Oswald can assasinate President Kennedy, I almost did not bother to buy it. But I am delighted I did. I should never have doubted the master. Once he began to weave his magic, I was lost. No point in summarising the plot; many reviewers have done that already. But the imagination that went into King's pseudo-scientific examination of time-travel and its effect on the traveller are weirdly convincing. The personification of Time itself into an implacable enemy attempting to derail the hero's plans is horrifying.<br /><br />These, and many other elements of the novel described by other reviewers, make 11.22.63 compulsive reading. But it is his extraordinarilly detailed and utterly accurate evocation of the 50s and 60s, the intense research that went into presenting the era of his own childhood with such nostalgic precision,, the images and sounds and tastes of those times so lovingly recreated, that make this book, for me, one of his best<br /><br />I am around King's age and I lived every second of his trip back to those gentle times. Oh, they were times of hardship, poverty, rationing...but there was so much trust in the general population in those days, so little of the horror and evil that blights much of today's world. And I smile when I see again through King's eyes the 50's cars with their huge gull wings, the 'new look' clothes, the kids congregating in the local pharmacy/cum ice-cream stores with their 'snugs' and juke-boxes. I lived it and I loved it and King has brought it all back. What a wonderful book..and, I hasten to add, what a wonderful story. Read it. You'll love it!

1936608618|4.0|For years the fitness industry has thrived on a \\"complicate to profit\\" model, in which so-called professionals attempt to persuade aspiring athletes or those just looking to get in shape that they need to follow some arcane, mysterious plan that exceeds the limits of their comprehension. It's turned into a bit of a shark tank, and one could easily be misled into doing either ineffective or worse, destructive training. Power Speed Endurance cuts through the BS and delivers exhaustive, exacting, and effective analysis of the skills and techniques required for swimming, biking, and running. While this isn't necessarily revolutionary, given that Colm O'Connell has been coaching runners in Kenya with an emphasis on sharpening their technique, and both Arthur Lydiard and Percy Cerutty worked with their athletes on running more effectively (each coach trained innumerable World & Olympic champions), it's been a long time since anyone's made this the centerpiece of their philosophy. And I've never seen such a thorough collection of all the drills, skills, and analysis of what works and what doesn't as MacKenzie and his team have compiled here. As an avid endurance athlete who's been fortunate to work with coaches who understand the technical side, I can say unequivocally that this book gives spectacular, easily-followed advice that will make you faster and spare you from injury. Get the iPad or print version, because you'll want access to all the pictures and diagrams--they are excellent. It is comprehensive, deeply researched, and presented brilliantly and vividly.<br /><br />While I don't agree that CrossFit Endurance's emphasis on WODs and intervals are optimal for improving performance in any endurance sport, they're still far better than the unstructured approach many people wind up taking. It's pretty well-established--despite what many CrossFit acolytes might argue, though some have toned it down lately--that properly paced \\"long slow distance\\" training, periodized and punctuated with hard intervals and anaerobic work produces the best results. Stephen Seiler's meta-study on endurance athletes across the major sports (running, cycling, swimming, rowing, Nordic skiing) demonstrates as much. And in my own experience, that was certainly the case because I didn't start winning races against higher-level athletes until I upped the volume and got smart about the intensity. But not everyone really has that sort of time, or desire, to do that much volume. Thus, for someone who's been doing nothing but long \\"medium hard\\" runs and rides, who rarely if ever does any meaningful anaerobic work, CrossFit Endurance will deliver results. It works best for those who've established something of an aerobic base. Of course, it's not like an elite-level athlete won't benefit either. Many endurance athletes are structurally imbalanced or simply not strong enough, and CFE provides a framework to correct yourself and improve that's better than pretty much anything else.<br /><br />Bottom line? If you'd like to get faster, and get stronger, buy this book, read it, and keep it as reference. If you're an elite or aspiring elite, you'll still have to do the volume, but improving your skills and getting stronger will go a long way for you.<br /><br />My background: Masters-level rower, 2011 bronze medal in the single scull at the Masters National Championships, casual triathlete, avid track cyclist, cyclocross & criterium racer.

B0084BMUFA|4.0|Realizing I had never read anything by Charles Dickens, I decided to take a crack at this novella after I found it being offered for free on Amazon. If you've seen any of the dozens of adaptations of this classic, you know the story: A cruel miser is visited by ghosts of past, present and future on Christmas Eve and given a glimpse of the misery he has and will continue to cause if he doesn't change his ways.<br /><br />As far as the Dickensian style goes, I can see the reasons why it is both loved and hated. At times the tone is conversational, like Dickens is standing in front of you telling the story, which is something you're either going to love or hate. At times I got a little lost in the text, but I just see that as a byproduct of reading a book that was written in a very different time and place.<br /><br />That said, the themes are very familiar, especially in today's political climate. Scrooge's dialogue in the opening scene could easily be adapted for one of the talking heads of Fox News. I'm not trying to get political here. Scrooge is a worst case scenario cartoon rendering of a heartless rich guy, but then again, I feel like he sees the error of his ways just a little too easily.<br /><br />At the end of the day, what you have in A Christmas Carol is a modern fable, and unlike many cultural myths, the original text is here for your enjoyment and analysis. This book is responsible in large part for how we experience and imagine Christmas today, so the cultural themes are vital. It's also a short read, so you won't lose a lot of time seeing if you like it.

B0041KVW2K|4.0|I received my box set and have so far listened to over half of it.  I had pressing issues as other have mentioned.  I called customer service and they 2 day shipped a replacement.  Great customer service!  If anyone has issues buying this because of the quality, Amazon will have you back so you can get a perfect set.<br /><br />As far as the vinyl itself, you need to be aware that these were not made to sound like original UK 1960s pressings.  If you want that sound then buy the original albums.  This box set is for people who do not have UK pressings and looking to get them in one set.  You should also know that the volume is quite a bit lower than original pressings.  They used 24/44.1 files WITHOUT limiting.  Turning the volume up will improve the sound some.  Having most of the original UK albums and other ones from around the world, I have heard details in the music I had never heard before.  Not having heard the original master tapes, I could only imagine this is the closest thing to being able to hear them.  Hearing new details in the music is worth the price for me.<br /><br />The sleeves and labels are not 100% reproductions.  I will say the artwork is very clear and great to look at.  Some may think the sleeves are too flimsy; I do not have a big issue with them.  I only have three complaints.  I wish they would have kept Beatles for Sale closer to the original by having the disk load from inside the gatefold sleeve.  Likewise, I wish The Beatles (White Album) was a top loader like the original.  They did a good job making the inner sleeves black for the white album and beyond, but I was hoping that they would have reproduced the EMTEX sleeves.  As far as the book included, no complaints here.  I enjoyed looking through it.<br /><br />This box set is not for everyone, but is certainly is not a waste of money.  If you have many of the original UK albums, you may not want this, however if you are new to Beatles vinyl (or only have US copies) this is a greatset.  Don't let all the negativity stop you from getting this.

1400063736|4.0|I was thrilled to receive an advance reader's copy of LET THE GREAT WORLD SPIN three and a half years ago but somehow it never made it to the top of my \\"to be read\\" pile until this week.  I have always been fascinated by New York City and Irish writer Colum McCann does a good job describing both the place and the very specific time period in which the novel takes place.  He tells his slice of life story from the viewpoint of several very diverse people who are somehow connected to each other on August 7, 1974 the day a young Frenchman walked across a tightrope spanning the two world trade center towers.  A saintly Irish member of a religious order, his visiting brother, a couple of Bronx prostitutes, some moms mourning the losses of their sons in Viet Nam, the judge who arraigns the tightrope walker and an artist from a wealthy family who has squandered much of her twenties on drugs and wild parties are some of the most memorable characters who are all interconnected in surprising ways.  McCann is able to give credible voices to these people including three African American women.  I also like the way McCann uses a very specific real life incident to anchor the stories of his fictional characters.  And McCann has a lot to say about social justice and problems that still exist in American society.  Yet I still feel this ambitious book doesn't quite live up to what it could have been.

1401937519|3.0|I appreciated Anita Moorjani's account of her NDE, but otherwise I wasn't crazy about this book, and I find some of its teachings at best factually incorrect, and at worst, dangerous.<br /><br />Anita Moorjani is a lovely human being. In her videos on youtube and in the glancing interactions I've witnessed on the web, she is unfailingly giving, open, generous of herself and her time, and eager to be of service to humanity. Some gurus have an air of smarm about them, boosting their own egos at their followers' expense. Some seem too quick to make a buck. Some are harsh and humiliate those who follow them. Anita Moorjani is a genuine and enthusiastic lady who is helping others because that is her path.<br /><br />\\"Dying to Be Me\\" has provided profound hope, liberation, and possibly even healing to hundreds, if not thousands of readers. Many of those readers have taken the inspiration they received from this book and used it to spread love and support to others.<br /><br />Anita Moorjani is of Hindu, Indian descent. She was born in Singapore and raised in Hong Kong. She was diagnosed with cancer and came within hours of what her doctors assumed would be an inevitable death.<br /><br />At that point, Anita had what she has referred to as a Near Death Experience, but what could more accurately be referred to as an out-of-body experience. Unlike others who have NDEs, Anita did not actually die. She entered a coma.<br /><br />Anita encountered her deceased father and her friend Soni, who had died of cancer. She also experienced ESP, spontaneously accessing the thoughts and conversations of widely dispersed friends, family, and physicians. After her OBE, Anita experienced a spontaneous remission of her cancer.<br /><br />What didn't work for me in Anita's book was the insistence that several postulates are true, postulates that I don't see any evidence to support.<br /><br />Anita reports that we are all God, and if you don't like the word \\"God\\" you can say Source or Allah or Brahma or any other word. We all create the universe. We attract into our lives the events we desire. We exercise control over illnesses like cancer. There is no difference between good and bad. There is no punishment in the afterlife. Duality is a bad thing. Religion is a bad thing because its prominent influence is to cause people to kill each other. We should do what makes us happy. Service should not be done out of duty, only if it makes us feel good.<br /><br />Too, Anita takes a few subtle swipes at Christianity and Western civilization. The nuns who taught her are subtly put down, as is Mother Teresa. English, as a language, is said to be so lacking that her NDE can't be communicated; cancer is a Western disease.<br /><br />Anita makes no attempt to foist these ideas on anyone. Unfortunately some have adopted these ideas as dogma, and insist that they are the only truth. By what authority? Anita has been to heaven, these folks insist; therefore, she must be correct.<br /><br />As it happens, though, many people have had NDEs at this point, and not everyone agrees with Anita. People do come back from the dead and report the punishment and judgment that Anita and her followers insist never occurs. Some have had NDEs of Hell, and insist on this emphatically. Others encounter Jesus, and return to human life with a greater conviction than ever that Jesus is savior.<br /><br />Some of these ideas, when carried to extremes - and people do carry them to extremes - are spiritually toxic. No, I am NOT God, and neither are you. Admitting that we are not God is one of the first steps in psychological maturity, and spiritual growth. Religion is not just about killing; that false belief is a tool for atheist propaganda. As a feminist and a woman I am mindful of, and grateful for, the elevation in the position of women brought about by Christianity. Before Christianity, as historian Rodney Stark has described, female infanticide was a constant of the Classical Greco-Roman world. Not killing female babies, and allowing women some choice and autonomy, was one of the reasons Christianity grew as fast as it did. I'm grateful for agriculture, universities, science, art, and the abolition of slavery, all of which are intertwined with the spread and growth of Christianity.<br /><br />No, we do not give ourselves cancer, and, no, if we just enjoy life and \\"follow our bliss\\" what we want will not fall into our laps. This idea is not a reflection of heaven, but, rather, of Anita's rather sheltered life and upper socio-economic class. It is an idea that will be attractive to other sheltered, economically comfortable people, eliminating, as it does, the need for compassion for the less fortunate.<br /><br />No, it does not contribute to spiritual growth to avoid service work or to do it only when it feels good to do so, and no, Mother Teresa did not deserve Anita's subtle put down. In my own life I have discovered firsthand that \\"bring the body and the heart will follow\\" is a key tenet of service to others. When I was tender with my dying patients even when I was not in the mood to be tender, my patients' appreciation lifted *me* up.<br /><br />No, goodness and badness are not the exact same thing and indistinguishable from each other. Discernment is one of the universally acclaimed features of spiritual maturity. Being able to differentiate between lies and truth, stealing and giving, hurting and healing, is essential. Hitler really was a very bad guy and the difference between him and - oh, say Mother Teresa - is profound. To equate the two is a spiritual step back.<br /><br />Anita decries duality, while practicing it. Religion is bad, service out of a sense of duty is bad, etc. You can't make pronouncements like this without relying on the concept of duality.n world. B008NBEY3C|5.0|I can think of no living musician on Sony Classical's roster more deserving of tribute than pianist Murray Perahia.  Since 1972, he's recorded a broad swath of the repertoire at a consistently high level.  No doubt, Sony also appreciates Perahia's loyalty to the label and its predecessor, Columbia (later CBS) Masterworks.<br /><br />Today, many think of Perahia, 65 at the time of this writing, as a Mozart and Bach specialist. But looking over the sequence of recordings as originally issued, his initial concentration was on Schumann, Chopin, and Mendelssohn.  Perahia's style evolved over the years: after the first ardent recordings of the early Romantics, he adopted a more conservative style as evidenced by his series of self-conducted Mozart concertos (still my favorite complete set of these works); In the 1990s, Perahia expanded into more extroverted repertoire and a virtuosic performing style, before hand injuries - not performance related - forced a withdrawal from public performances, a time Perahia used to study Bach.  Anyone with doubts as to the current state of Perahia's technique need only listen to the most recent recording in the set: Brahms' Handel Variations.  (According to an interview in the accompanying book, Perahia is now planning to record Bach's French Suites.)  At every stage of his evolution, Perahia has demonstrated a rare balance in his playing: he's able to convey the structure of the music without sounding dryly analytical; his technique is solid but never meretricious; his tone is lovely without being an end in itself; the emotional temperature of his playing seems \\"just right\\" for each piece.<br /><br />In addition to Perahia's complete Columbia/Sony recordings, a 1967 recording on the Vox label is included, and five DVD's.  In all matters of presentation, Sony has gotten things right.  The original album format recreates the sequence of the LPs and, later, CDs - the original jacket covers and labels are also reproduced.  As a result, the playing time for the first decade's worth of albums is short, but at the budget price the set's offered at, why fret?  I'd rather have short playing times than clumsily sequenced compilation albums.  The beautifully illustrated book contains track information, an index of compositions, and the complete English language liner notes for each album (in addition, a website address is provided with the notes in French and German).<br /><br />All recordings made prior to 1989 have been newly remastered, with pronounced improvement in the sound quality: the strident treble that was heard on early CD issues has been tamed, and the dynamic range has been increased - yielding a warmer, more vibrant sound picture. Of course, that's all speculative on my part.  However, if glued-binding is the only way this volume is produced, then shame on you, Marvel.  This is your signature event of the year, and you have spat in the face of anyone who has paid the $75.00 cover price, or any price for that matter.  Furthermore, you have taken what appears to be brilliant artwork by Kubert, Coipel and Romita Jr., and marred it. As a life-long Marvel fan, this has to be the only disappointment I've truly experienced in purchasing a trade volume.  A big one at that.  2 stars, \\"I don't like it\\".

0525953728|5.0|Let me start off by saying that \\"No Easy Day\\" is a great book and one of the best I have ever read. Although the title is \\"the firsthand account of the killing of Osama Bin Laden\\", it isn't really about that. It is mostly about the time in the Navy Seals, from 1998 when he was in BUD/S (basic underwater demolition/seals) training all the way up to when he was a team leader on the mission that killed Bin Laden. Usually when I read a book I will read a chapter or two and then put the book down, but with this book I couldn't put it down because I wanted to see what would happen next and couldn't wait until the next night.<br /><br />The author was able to make the book very interesting for some things that would usually sound boring. He really shows you are the much training and work he had to do to get to where he was. He emphasizes that when they had to do a mission that could take as little as a half hour, they had to do weeks of training, and for the Bin Laden mission, they had to do months of training in a replica of the house for a mission that was supposed to take only 38 minutes. Also, the author talks about some of the struggles he went through. For example, during training, everyone had to make a list of the five best and worst in training, which the training leader would use to make the weekly cuts. Another example is the time you have to be away from home and not being able to tell your family where you are, what you are doing or when you will be home.<br /><br />In addition to the interesting parts, there were also many parts that lacked in suspense. Some of that is shown when he is in training. The author would go on about how they had to go through the \\"kill house\\" so many times a day and that sometimes there would be high ranking people watching. Also, while waiting for the order to do the Bin Laden mission, he was just waiting around and talking to the other guys.<br /><br />To sum it all up, this is one of the greatest books I have ever read and i would recommend it to anyone who likes a good and suspenseful action/adventure book.

B008KGPEQG|4.0|Grade: B<br /><br />L/C Ratio: 80/20<br />(This means I estimate the author devoted 80% of his effort to creating a literary work of art and 20% of his effort to creating a commercial bestseller.)<br /><br />Thematic Breakdown:<br />50% - Memoir<br />20% - Aging<br />15% - Family<br />10% - New York<br />  5% - Paris<br /><br />I first got hooked on Paul Auster's writing when I read City of Glass in a college English course. Ever since then, I've found him to be one of the most innovative and engaging voices in modern American literature. For the most part, that genius carries over into his latest work of nonfiction, Winter Journal.<br /><br />There are two things that elevate Winter Journal and differentiate it from standard memoir fare. The first is the narrative approach. Auster employs the second person throughout the book, and the focus on \\"you\\" really draws the reader into the text. The other key element is Auster's choice of anecdotes. He devotes very little of Winter Journal to talking about his own books or his writing career. There are no stories about publishing deals or book signings or crazy literary fans. Instead, he turns the memoir into a fascinating study of himself as a human being and a reflection of his own aging process.<br /><br />Winter Journal has moments of pure poetry, but the strength of Auster's commentary fades a bit down the stretch. Some of his techniques (such as describing a poignant moment from each house he's ever lived in) work well, while others (such as the long list of foods he enjoyed as a child) are unnecessary. But Winter Journal succeeds in the most important way - it fosters an intimate connection between the reader and author.<br /><br />Noteworthy Quote:<br /><br />You think it will never happen to you, that it cannot happen to you, that you are the only person in the world to whom none of these things will ever happen, and then, one by one, they all begin to happen to you, in the same way they happen to everyone else.

Where There are No Holes! B006IYFCIM|5.0|I loved Dan's first two books, but this one feels like a rush to publish more books than Malcolm Gladwell.  While his conclusions about individual and group honesty resonate on an intuitive basis, most of the \\"experiments\\" used to build his behavioral models are disconnected from the real world in which decisions about honest and dishonest actions are made.  The experiments, set in universities, put the subjects into inconsequential environments in which unimportant tasks are performed.  While Dan might argue that a person's underlying sense of honesty manifest itself in all environments, his own thesis that \\"moral reminders\\" shape behavior, is largely ignored in extrapolating from the lab to the real world.  I am not suggesting people are more or less honest than Dan suggests, I don't think his experiments provide a solid basis to draw his conclusions.  Dan's writing style makes the book an easy and enjoyable read, but that is not sufficient to make it good social science.

0340364777|1.0|I'm a huge Stephn King fan (particularly of some his short stories, such as his masterwork, \\"N\\"), but \\"It\\" is his absolute worst.  Why:<br /><br />1.  It's NOT SCARY.  There's a bit of creepiness in the beginning of the opening scene, when six-year-old George describes his fears of the cellar and then nevertheless forces himself to reach into the darkness to flip the switch, but once the NON-SCARY clown shows up and starts chatting with George from inside a sewer drain, the unknown has been made known, concrete and even a bit silly.  What the clown does to kill George is horrific, yes, and comes as a surprise, but it's not SCARY.  There's a difference.  The best horror -- including *King's* best horror -- is not only horrific but scary, better yet terrifying.  That King quotes repeatedly from Shirley Jackson's masterful, unforgettable \\"Haunting of Hill House\\" only highlights how far short \\"It\\" falls of that mark, and comes across as arrogance and ego on King's part, a curious blindness to his own work's gross inferiority.<br /><br />The first leper scene was a bit creepy, but then, come, on!  The Teenage Werewolf?  Frankenstein?  Those hokey, corny, even *campy* monsters from 1950s matinees that scared, if anyone, only the under-nine crowd?  I kept thinking, What next?  Mothra?  Count Chocula?<br /><br />And to top it all off, at the end, it turns out that the fearsome shape-shifting \\"It,\\" in its \\"true\\" form, is just a Big Ol' Spider???  A pregnant spider (you know, like Charlotte in Charlotte's Web) whose prematurely delivered babies can be killed just be stomping on them?  A spider that can be killed by having its heart squeezed between the hero's two hands?  I kept thinking of the spider in Richard Matheson's wonderful \\"The Shrinking Man\\" -- *that* was a spider to contend with.  (And that was a book that not only was decidedly creepy, it also, in marked contrast to King's mess, had something to say about the human condition.)  \\"Pet Sematery\\" was SCARY, in a way that has stayed with me since I was a teenager.  The scene in which the cat jumps off the bed and lands with an ungainly lurch is unforgettable.  \\"It\\" failed utterly in this regard.<br /><br />2.  The group sex scene among 11-year-olds is not only, as many others have pointed out, gratuitous (i.e., you could easily excise it from the book and do nothing to disturb or weaken the plot, themes, pacing and characterizations; in fact, in this case you could *improve* all of them) and based on the fallacious non-logic about children and sex favored by pedophiles (I'll explain that below), but also bathetically, risibly awful, the literary equivalent of an Elvis painting on black velvet.  It's bathetic primarily because it's so *unreal,* so inexplicable in terms of Beverley's character, and those of all the boys, and because it therefore comes across as too convenient; an authorly self-indulgence.  In short, a *sexual fantasy* introduced to serve the author's desires (and that would presumably pander to those of some readers), rather than something reality-based that would logically grow out of the specific characters and the situation in which they found themselves at the time.<br /><br />King could've vastly improved the scene if he had, say, made it clear that Bev was an extremely screwed-up kid who was not only a sexual masochist (even at age 11), but also was defiantly, and in a sick way, fulfilling her father's prurient and insulting assumption that she was having sex with her male friends at a time when their friendships were entirely sex-free; that doing it in the sewer made it all the dirtier and more disgusting for her, because that's how her father had made her feel about herself.  (If, iow, he'd done the basic writer's job of truly entering the character's head and making his or her actions logical in terms of character.)  And if he'd presented it as Bad Sex and a Bad Thing; as an instance of a psychologically abused and confused girl pressuring a bunch of equally confused boys into an uncomfortable-to-traumatic-to-scarring event for boys and girl alike, which they either never discussed again out of shame and mortification, or, more likely, eventually led to one or more of the boys, right after Bev made him angry or jealous, spreading the story all around school to publicly humiliate and shame her.  But, no, King presents the group sex as if it's sweet, beautiful, healthy and even cosmic.  As if Bev was RIGHT that it was necessary for her to have sex with all the boys in order for them to escape from the maze of the sewers after killing the Big Ol' Spider.<br /><br />The scene also fails to be true to the character of the boys.  For example, would sensitive, self-conscious-about-his-fat-body Ben, who has an intense crush on Bev, be willing to have sex with his beloved for the first time *in front of 5 other 11-year-old boys*??  Would he be okay with sharing his beloved with these five others in a gang bang?  In a sewer??  I don't think so, and I think anyone who's ever gone through a childhood crush would agree.<br /><br />As for the pedophile-logic part, while it's true that the scene itself isn't pedophilic, since all the participants are 11-year-olds, it *does* rely on several lies about kids and sex that pedophiles routinely trot out as rationalizations for their crimes.  The lie that it's plausible for an 11-year-old girl to initiate sex, even pressure the boy(s) into it, out of simple, healthy desire.  The lie that an 11-year-old understands the social meaning and nature of sex sufficiently to be capable of informed consent to it.  The lie that it's plausible for an 11-year-old girl to have sex for the first time with six boys, one after another, suffer only a bit of blood loss and soreness, enjoy it and even have an orgasm.  This portrayal has nothing to do with real 11-year-old girls, and everything to do with the fantasy girls dreamed of by pedophiles.  (See A.M. Holmes's deeply flawed but still superior \\"End of Alice\\" for an example of a unreliable pedophile narrator who tries to fob off his fantasy of a sexually voracious 13-year-old girl as real, when in fact she was unwilling, unconscious and his rape and murder victim.)<br /><br />3.  Underlying sexism, racism and heterosexism/hetero-normism.  Isn't it odd how we've got a group of seven kids/adults, one of which is black, one of which is Jewish, and one of which is female, but it ends up that it's three of the white, Christian, macho guys who are supposedly mentally and physically strong enough to face down the Big Spider and kill it?  The Jewish guy is the one who's so mentally fragile that he commits suicide in one of the first scenes.  The black guy is peeled off early by a mere \\"dogsbody\\" of It and sent to the hospital with knife wound.  Another one of the white Christian guys -- but one who's been portrayed throughout as a weak Mama's Boy who's now dominated by his wife -- is unlucky enough to have his arm bitten off by the Spider in a preliminary skirmish and dies.  The female character -- the oh-so-wiling-to-conform-to-the-male-author's-fantasies-and-convenience Bev -- chooses to sit back and cradle the head of the dead Mama's Boy in her lap.  Ben, the fat boy who grew up to be thin, tough, successful and a man with a gargantuan ability to hold his liquor, is given the secondary role of stomping on the Spider's babies.  It's left to the group's leader, Bill, and its successful class-clown-type, Richie -- both straight, white and male -- to confront the monster directly and kill it.<br /><br />The only gay characters are early victims of It -- unless you want to count the two bully Bad Guys who toy with a half-fulfilled exchange of hand jobs before one stomps off in a huff.  And of course, when we have the group sex that's supposed to cosmically deepen the bond of the group, it has to be exclusively heterosexual.  Six guys on the one girl, no guy-on-guy sex at all, as if it's unthinkable, even for cosmic-style sex.<br /><br />4. Literary matters.  *Way* too long and repetitious with indulgent tangents and digressions.  Boring.  The structure -- switching from one of the seven characters to another, sub-chapter by sub-chapter creates a sense of a writer going through the motions, or ticking off the steps in the story as if it were a to-do list.  It makes the work as a whole feel uninspired, like it ain't got that \\"swing.\\"  At the same time the structure is overly complex, switching between third-person narrations and first-person \\"interludes\\" narrated by Mike, the black guy.  The constant shifting between 1958 and 1985 is also overly complex and at times hard to follow -- sometimes I had to flip back and check the heading to see which period we were in.  The device of having the characters \\"forget\\" everything that happened when they faced down It feels too convenient -- a way of saving the climactic scenes for the end.Alice\\" for an example of a unreliable pedophile narrator who tries to fob off his fantasy of a sexually voracious 13-year-old girl as real, when in fact she was unwilling, unconscious and his rape and murder victim.)<br /><br />3. It is powered by USB or the included AC adapter, and even has enough power for phantom powered microphones, which is perfect for my compression mic. The instrument cable and microphone XLR input are in the same place, so you can only use one at a time, but it's great for a one man band with one-by-one multitrack recording methods. There are bass, treble and pan bars for both channels, but I've only ever used the main channel and haven't tried using the line out as of yet. The sound is excellent and crystal clear once you get it completely set up.<br /><br />This mixer is pretty basic, and you won't find a lot of fancy features. But if you're looking to make professional sounding recordings, with some practice and tinkering, this is perfect. Having this, I really have no need for any other recording equipment besides my PC and some mulitrack software. The price is amazing for making the dream of turning my bedroom into a recording studio come true. I'm not sure how this would fare with live performances because you would have to do some rigging to run multiple instruments through it and it might be a hassle, but I think it's perfect for home recording.<br /><br />As a few other reviewers mentioned, you really need to go to the Behringer website and download the drivers for this mixer. It will clear up the hiss and buzz that you hear when you record with it straight out of the box, and it makes all the difference in the world quality wise. I use Audacity with ASIO plugins to record my audio, which requires me to crank up the volume and gain a lot to get the sound to a good level, but after some tinkering, you'll get very clear sound with no audible buzz or hiss, even using a microphone.l, but after some tinkering, you'll get very clear sound with no audible buzz or hiss, even using a microphone.
B009439HEY|4.0|I have to start by saying I'm a huge Hackett fan, and consider him to be one of the finest guitarists alive, particularly because of his amazing versatility (world-class classical technique, plus killer rock chops, plus amazing songwriting and arranging abilities).  SO - why only 4 stars?  It's beacuse I found these remakes to be \\"too\\" perfect -- that is, they are for the most part note-for-note replications of the originals.  This produces a strangely 3-sided effect:  1) it makes you marvel at how GOOD this music inherently is, as music;  2)  it makes you marvel at how he could have accomplished this feat (getting all the tones right, the parts right, the layering/mix right); and  3) It makes you yearn for some kind of \\"new spin\\" -- why go to all this trouble just to so-closely duplicate these performances, when there is so much \\"meat\\" to the music itself, which would have allowed him so much freedom of expression for new arrangements?  This is really a dilemma for me -- on the one hand, Bravo Steve!!  But on the other hand, WHY didn't he take this opportunity to breathe some fresh air into the music?  The attention to detail is astonishing (in \\"Ripples\\" for example, as the song is fading out, there's that memorable syncopated drum toms fill that leads back to the downbeat - a tiny detail, but he remembered to include it).  It's awesome - but, it begs for Steve to have used his amazing virtuosity to make the songs - say - 10% different -- keep the core sound, but add new textures or arranging ideas.<br /><br />The only true criticism I'd make is the last track, \\"Shadow of the Hierophant\\".  Maybe it's just me, but wow is the tempo fast on this one!  If it had been a new/different arrangement, then the faster tempo wouldn't have been a problem, because then \\"the whole thing\\" would have been different.  But the arrangement is identical to the original, yet noticably faster -- this is jarring, and \\"wrong\\"-sounding.  But I'm nitpicking -- the album is great - it just could have been a little greater.  I will say this -- on the day that Steve Hackett is gone, the world will be a hell of a less interesting place.

0316017930|4.0|In Outliers, Gladwell challenges the traditional explanations of success and how the successful tell their stories. He asserts that there is no such thing as personal explanations of success; he opposes how the successful of our time refers to themselves as self-made men without any mention of the stream of opportunity that came across their ways. To put his points into practice, Gladwell tells stories of successful people like Bill Joe, Steve Jobs, Bill Gates, etc. and points out the opportunities these people came across with played a major role in shaping their success.<br /><br />One idea resonates across every chapter in the book; the idea that luck and arbitrary advantages are the main determinants of success in the world we're living in today. He argues that our date of birth, where we grew up, what family we belong to, and the people we meet in our life make a big difference in our path to success - these factors are all out of our control.<br /><br />However, he also argues that it is not always about luck; he acknowledges that hard work does play a role in one's success. He says that no one who wakes up before dawn in the morning and work much harder fails to make his family rich. My favorite quote from the book is: \\"before you can become an expert, someone has to give you the chance to learn how to become an expert\\"; Gladwell mentions this in the book to point out that even the hard workers need to be given the opportunity to unleash their potentials before they can become successful. If you ask yourself this question: what really makes a difference between two equally intelligent people? The answer will read: the number of opportunities they've had in their life. Gladwell concludes that what really determine success are the opportunities that come along one's life and the ability to make use of them.<br /><br />I recommend this book to anyone interested in sociology, psychology, or just reading in general. By reading this book, you'll become one of thosepeople, like me, whose understanding of success has been changed in a way you cannot even begin to imagine.

0062014102|3.0|I tend to be skeptical about Grand Theories of Everything, and I do not trust studies that simply lump every anecdote, rumor, legend, or alleged \\"scientific study\\" in one big pile to confirm such a theory.  Well, that's what Talbot's book is.  It's a little depressing to read these reviews and see how uncritical some people are because they want so hard to believe their New Age dogmas.<br /><br />That said, however, the book is worth at least a casual read, because what he discusses is certainly worth thinking about.  And considering he wrote this over twenty years ago, just before his premature death, some of what he hypothesizes seems prescient in light of cosmological and astrophysical discoveries since that time, especially concerning dark matter, dark energy, and the shape, origins, and expansion of the universe.  (Note: I am not by any means an expert in either field, although I have a strong interest and have done some serious reading in both.)  The holographic concept deserves serious consideration, even if it is ultimately untestable.  I also agree with his idea of the connection between science and spirituality--I admire the Dalai Lama's thoughts on that issue--and his holistic approach to scientific exploration.<br /><br />But he goes overboard.  He let his enthusiasm run away with him.  For example, although I tend to agree with mind-body theories of disease, his chapter on cancer--especially what he has to say about snake oil like Krebiozen--is way over the top.  He treats every mystical experience anyone ever had as absolutely valid.  He applies his Grand Theory uncritically to NDE's, dreams, religious visions, psychic phenomena...you name it.  In short, he completely abandons the logical rigor and skepticism that is needed in a serious study that purports to be scientific.  Perhaps had he lived longer, he might have been able to develop his ideas in a more well-organized fashion.<br /><br />So read it.  The first third is worth a careful read for a good introduction to the ideas of David Bohm.  The last two-thirds--give it a good skim, and keep an open mind.

B0083I90HU|4.0|Sarah's Key follows the story of Julia Jarmond, an American journalist living in France, who is assigned to complete an article on the Vel' d'Hiv' roundup in France as well as the story of Sarah, a young girl, who experienced the roundup first hand.  The novel explores France's role in World War II and shines light on an otherwise scarcely remembered tragedy of the Holocaust.  While most novels about the Holocaust delve into its horrors, Sarah's Key delves into the effects of the Holocaust that are still felt decades later by the French.<br /><br />The novel alternates between Julia's point of view and Sarah's point of view, switching from first person to third person respectively.  Julia's story encompasses some issues and real-life problems that seem truly superficial as compared to the tragedy of Sarah's life.  There is a parallel between the two stories that enhances the reading experience and connects them together.  Julia's story, however, dominates the tail end of the novel, much to my displeasure and leaves Sarah's story left feeling incomplete.  While Julia does eventually piece the story together from her perspective, it loses the impact that it might have had if it had come from Sarah's point of view.<br /><br />Overall, Sarah's Key is a good read, but it does tend toward the female demographic with a \\"mid-wife\\" kind of narrative on Julia's end.  It illuminates a part of history that many are not aware of and provides some insight into the role of the French during the Holocaust.  If you like tear-jerking, emotional stories about the Holocaust, Sarah's Key is definitely for you.

Some wag, I don't remember who, opined that \\"the other six critics are all from New York City.\\" Breakfast at Tiffany's, Blake Edwards' love song to that same city, has an 88% rating at Rotten Tomatoes, and I think it's the opposite--the other twelve are from outside the five boroughs. Don't get me wrong, it's a very pretty thing, and the combination of Truman Capote's wit coming through Blake Edwards' lens, back in the days when Blake Edwards was still doing material that could be considered edgy (i.e., before his directorial career became an endless sequence of family-oriented Pink Panther sequels) produces some nasty laughs indeed. But is that enough to not only carry the movie, but the incredible amount of baggage that's attached itself to the movie during the past half-century?<br /><br />Plot: Holly Golightly (Audrey Hepburn in the role that defined her career) is a socialite who, as the film opens, gets a new downstairs neighbor, Paul Varjak (How the West Was Won`s George Peppard), a writer-cum-gigolo installed in the place by his sugar mama (The Day the Earth Stood Still`s Patricia Neal), who lives down the hall. The two strike up a friendship that might turn into a romance were it not for Holly's desperate desire to marry up; she's got her sights set on Rusty Trawler (Lilies of the Field`s Stanley Adams), \\"the ninth-richest man in America under the age of fifty\\".<br /><br />...and that's it, really, with an extra subplot or two added in (Paul is a writer with one critically-acclaimed but commercially-unviable book under his belt, Holly has a past she doesn't like to think about, etc.). To modern eyes, the most terrifying piece of the film is Mickey Rooney's cartoonish, stereotypical portrayal of Holly's Japanese upstairs neighbor, Mr. Yunioshi, which is offensive at every turn. Audiences in 1961 probably found it hysterical while shielding the kiddies' eyes from the idea of a young woman living on her own and doing relatively well at it (while Varjak is explicitly a gigolo, Holly's status as a high-class prostitute remains intentionally ambiguous, but in terms of 1961 dollars, did anyone really believe her \\"dates\\" were giving her fifty bucks to go to the powder room? Not unless she was going to the one with the wrong plumbing).<br /><br />It's true that the final scene is a masterpiece of emotional manipulation (but let's be honest here: we're far more concerned about the fate of Cat than we are whether Holly is finally going to let her feelings for Paul break through), but it's far from enough to get through the ponderous, badly-aged first hour and three-quarters of the movie to get to unless you have the same love affair with New York City as did Truman Capote and Blake Edwards. **sterical while shielding the kiddies' eyes from the idea of a young woman living on her own and doing relatively well at it (while Varjak is explicitly a gigolo, Holly's status as a high-class prostitute remains intentionally ambiguous, but in terms of 1961 dollars, did anyone really believe her \\"dates\\" were giving her fifty bucks to go to the powder room? I have a large Beatles record collection and what I think is a respectable audio system, Von Schweikert VR-4 JR MK 3, VPI Classic, Marsh but that's not important. I listened to Golden Slumbers and there was a lot of surface noise and three pops that occurred in the same places each time the cut was played. The audio sounded good but it was hard for me to get past the surface noise. These are not audiophile pressings by any stretch of the imagination.  I think the masters used had the potential to really sound good. I have a copy of Norah Jones Come Away With Me pressed by Quality Record Pressings on the Blue Note label (owned by EMI) and it's just phenomenal, zero surface noise. I have other Beatles pressings that are quiet and the only background noise you hear is the master tape hiss. That experience brings me much closer to the music than these pressings.  I'd still buy this set as I'm a collector but I probably won't listen to it.

George Martin's \\"Live and Let Die\\" was another spine tickler for Bond variations, not including his protg Paul McCartney's title nausea, (although chic at the time, Martin could have also toned back some of that silly slap bass). Without it, the Bond films would just be routine action movies.  With that said, why don't these composers realize this?<br /><br />There are three points to the Bond musical mystique.  The simple melody, the eternal four note Bond signature progression and of course, the Norman crescendo.  Without these, there is no Bond. Its like the powers to be are doing all thisjust to annoy their long time fans.  That logo is as important to the series as the hammer and stamp was in Dragnet.  Without it, there isn't! 0385343248|5.0|Most Historical fiction is historical fiction simply because it takes place in the past. Reading it doesnt teach you about other cultures, or a different time. Shogun is a book that teaches you a great deal about European history(Did you know that the Netherlands used to be a colony of Spain? I didn't), Japanese history, and Japanese culture. The novel does all this while weaving through an epic tale of political intrigue, and one mans journey to understand a foreign people.<br /><br />The book centers around an English captain, and his travels in Japan after getting shipwrecked there. He gets caught up in a battle for who will rule Japan, and grows to love and care for the Japanese culture, and see his own culture as barbaric in comparison. The personal journey of Blackthorne s slow and gradual, and almost unnoticed until the last pages. The book also follows the politics of succession in Japan after the death of the Shogun. Blackthorne's various captors all are involved, and its quite fascinating. The tale is quite reminiscent of \\"Game of Thrones\\", except with real Japanese history.<br /><br />The book is definitely not light reading. Its an intense tale with a lot of characters, and you can definitely get lost if your not paying attention closely. I would highly reccomend it for anyone.

B004J4WNL2|5.0|In \\"Quiet\\", Susan Cain examines life from the perspective of an introvert living in a world, or at least in a part of the world, that is structured to disproportionately favor the extrovert.  Before you conclude, \\"Well, of course.  That's just nature!\\" Mrs. Cain would have you consider the evidence, and answer a few questions.<br /><br />If extroversion is a dominant and preferable social temperament, then why is it not so in much of Asia?  If introversion is unhealthy and limiting, something to be overcome as we grow up, then why was it not that for Einstein, Gandhi, Newton, or Steve Wozniak?  If you were forced to choose (not that that's advisable or even possible) between the characteristics of `gregarious and popular' or `persistent and solitary' for your child, which would you choose?<br /><br />Reality is that anywhere from a third to a half of the people in western culture are possessed of an introverted temperament.  Half your employees.  Half your colleagues.  Half your students.  Have you considered that the environment in which you gather them for work, interaction, and study might put half of them at a huge creative disadvantage?  If you think that needs changing, and that the desirable change is that everyone should strive a little harder to become `happy, shiny people' because that's the best way to be, think again.  \\"Love\\", she says, \\"is essential; gregariousness is optional.\\"<br /><br />She further addresses and destroys - quietly and thoughtfully - commonly held misconceptions (and myths) about \\"what's wrong with\\" an introverted child or adult.  She offers practical advice - real strategies - for relationships, for raising an introverted child, for overcoming the fear of public interaction, and for arranging your office space to promote creativity among your staff.<br /><br />For those who need to know more about the nuts and bolts, she examines what I believe are vital questions: about brain chemistry and structure, about concepts like \\"high-reactivity\\", \\"self-monitoring\\", \\"free trait theory\\", and \\"optimal levels of arousal\\".  Biology and psychology were never this riveting in college.<br /><br />At the very least, she has leveled a - deliberate and reasoned - blow on behalf of those who would have words like \\"she's too sensitive for her own good\\" or \\"he shouldn't take things so close to heart\\" struck forever from the language.<br /><br />Mrs. Cain, wherever you have been hiding, thanks for speaking up for the quiet half. B000056HE9|5.0|I have my collection of Christmas movies that I watch every year.  Unfortunately, I had to watch The Bishop's Wife when it was shown on the TCM channel, however, I was thrilled when I was able to purchase it.  Cary Grant is an angel who arrives to help a Bishop (David Niven)realize that his ministry is not about grand schemes or huge cathedrals, but his ministry is about the simple things in life.  Loretta Young is the Bishop's wife and Cary Grant befriends her and through his friendship, she comes to realize that her husband needs to return to his simple way of ministry.  I am a lover of old movies and this one does not disappoint.  It is a really sweet, funny, and dramatic look at people and their faults and their goodness.  The acting is fabulous and seems so uncontrived.  Each of the stars are so perfect for their parts that it is hard to imagine that, at one time, David Niven wanted to play the part of the angel.  I think another reason why this movie is so wonderful is that it harkens back to a different time...when life was less stressful, less complicated.  It sends a message but not in an overt way and when it ended, I just got a good feeling.  I highly recommend adding this to your Christmas movie collection.
